    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="faab1e4acb5648fa9726468e76f15ccc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '669bcb2f-fc2b-48ba-896b-bfcc77bd03de'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d131054348" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) suffer from severe capacity decay as the harmful substances caused by the violent decomposition of electrolyte under high voltages continue to erode the cathodes. Therefore, the design of high-voltage electrolyte and construction of robust cathode–electrolyte interface (CEI) are critical for long-life SIBs. Herein, an electrically coupled composite electrolyte that takes the merits of cross-linked gel polymers and s well-tuned antioxidant additive (4-trifluoromethylphenylboronic acid, TFPBA) is proposed. Through an electrical coupling effect, TFPBA can be anchored by the cross-linked polymer framework to immobilize the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion and adsorb onto cathode surface spontaneously, both of which promote the formation of a robust CEI layer to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> transportation and suppress subsequent side reactions and corrosive cracking. As a result, the cells integrating high-voltage P2/O3 cathode and well-tailored gel polymer electrolyte achieve stable cycling over 550 cycles within 1.8–4.2&nbsp;V with a capacity retention of 71.0% and a high-rate discharge capacity of 77.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. The work paves the way for the development of functionalized quasi-solid electrolyte for practical next generation high-voltage SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307061-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>So far, the rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used as energy storage devices, but they also have sparked concerns of Li shortage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0001_R_d131054337e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered the ideal alternatives to LIBs that have experienced in a period of rapid development,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0002_R_d131054337e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> due to the low cost and ubiquitous resources of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0002_R_d131054337e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0003_R_d131054337e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Numerous researches have thus been focused on inexpensive SIBs pairing with high-theoretical-capacity layered oxides to achieve long cycle life and high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0004_R_d131054337e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the cycling stability of layered oxide cathodes strongly depends on the selection of electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0005_R_d131054337e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical decomposition of carbonate solvents (e.g., propylene carbonate [PC], ethyl methyl carbonate [EMC]), salts (e.g., sodium hexafluorophosphate [NaPF<sub>6</sub>])<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0006_R_d131054337e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Power Sources ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> triggered at high operating voltage severely deteriorates the performance and eventual lifespan of SIBs on account of the generation of hazardous byproducts such as hydrofluoric acid (HF). Typically, HF and other detrimental by-products will seriously corrode the cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) film and cathode materials, leading to the continuous consumption of electrolyte, the dissolution of transition metal (TM) ions, severe capacity fading and cell failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0007_R_d131054337e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Predictably, electrolyte chemistry, including additive strategy, high-concentration electrolytes, and ionic liquids, is an important approach to achieve high interfacial stability for SIBs. Among them, the additive strategy is one of the most flexible and cost-effective methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0008_R_d131054337e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Power Sources Energy Storage Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reactivity of most additives in electrolyte are incredibly challenging to control, and thus it is difficult to form a highly stable electrolyte system, inevitably resulting in the generation of excessive oxidation products or harmful by-products and deteriorating electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>As a response without affecting the electrochemical performance, gel polymer electrolytes (GPEs), which could enhance the stability and oxidation resistance of electrolyte systems,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0009_R_d131054337e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater. Chem Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have received growing attention as the usage of highly active additives. To be precise, small molecular monomers with functional groups are firstly screened and designed to immobilize free active species, and then obtain the GPE with strong framework and better interface compatibility by in situ polymerization, which can enable the batteries to achieve robust CEI film and excellent electrochemical performance. In addition, some polymer molecules can also, like organic solvents, sever as ligands to coordinate with Na<sup>+</sup> through functional groups (such as carbonyl groups (C═O) and ether groups (─O─)),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0010_R_d131054337e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which decreases the desolvation activation energy dramatically and regulates the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus promoting the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> and reducing interface side reactions.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by these considerations, an electrically coupled gel polymer electrolyte system (GPE+TFPBA), using ethoxylated trimethylolpropane triacrylate (ETPTA)-acetoacetoxyethyl methacrylate (AAEM) copolymer with electronegative groups as polymer network and the highly active 4-trifluoromethylphenylboronic acid (TFPBA) with electron acceptor function as additive, is fabricated by in situ free-radical polymerization. Theoretical calculations and detailed characterizations reveal that, based on the electrical coupling mechanism, the TFPBA can not only absorb spontaneously onto the cathode surface and anchor the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, but also achieve excellent oxidation stability through the strong immobilization within the P(ETPTA-AAEM) matrix, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0001">1b</a>. The synergy between two components thus leads to a highly stable gel electrolyte system with a wide electrochemical window up to 5.1&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), an optimal Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure and inhibited interfacial side reactions in high-voltage SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0001">1c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307061-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cccc6d72-5769-4009-a072-d2c1540767d1/adfm202307061-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cccc6d72-5769-4009-a072-d2c1540767d1/adfm202307061-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0edbc128-9eb8-4cd5-84d7-7aca8cbe9c58/adfm202307061-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cccc6d72-5769-4009-a072-d2c1540767d1/adfm202307061-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307061-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustrations of (a) the degradation process of pristine SIBs using liquid electrolyte (LE), (b) the electrically coupling engineering strategy and (c) the synergy effect of GPE+TFPBA composite electrolyte for SIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307061-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202307061-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design Principle and Physicochemical Characterizations of the Electrolytes</h3>
            
            <p>TFPBA molecule possesses special ─BOH groups, which exhibits electropositivity because B atom is sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized and owns an empty p-orbital.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0011_R_d131054337e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Coord. Chem. Rev. J. Membr. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, TFPBA can anchor anions (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2a</a>) and interact with lattice oxygen to adsorb onto the cathode surface spontaneously (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) via electrical coupling, which is also confirmed by the electrostatic potential analysis of TFPBA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, TFPBA with a higher highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) value (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2b</a>) is conducive to pre-oxidation to participate in the formation of CEI film. However, the preferential oxidation of modified liquid electrolyte (LE+TFPBA) occurs at only 3.2&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) and contributes to earlier severe oxidation at 4.0&nbsp;V, compared with that of liquid electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2c</a>). This observation infers that the highly reactive TFPBA has a seriously negative impact on chemical stability of liquid electrolyte. Hence, to achieve a highly stable electrolyte operated at high voltages, the GPE that can regulate the oxidation stability of TFPBA wa introduced. The preparation and detailed physicochemical characterizations of GPEs could be found in the Supporting Information and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S8</a> (Supporting Information). And as expected, benefiting from the strong electrical coupling effect between polymer framework and TFPBA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), the TFPBA that immobilized within modified gel polymer electrolyte (GPE+TFPBA) exhibits a much higher oxidation potential at ≈4.0&nbsp;V and the GPE+TFPBA also displays a wider electrochemical window of 5.1&nbsp;V. Moreover, the cells using GPE+TFPBA exhibit a higher Coulombic efficiency (CE) and excellent reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> plating/stripping process according to the ratio between reduction peak and oxidation peak (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information). Even though its overall ionic conductivity (1.89 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) is reduced compared to liquid electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), it can still meet the application requirements of electrolytes (&gt;1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0012_R_d131054337e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun. Joule">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and simultaneously its Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) improves significantly (0.7629, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which compensates mostly the loss of Na<sup>+</sup> conduction in GPE+TFPBA.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307061-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c4e6f487-2e98-4a67-8c73-11689e2940d7/adfm202307061-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c4e6f487-2e98-4a67-8c73-11689e2940d7/adfm202307061-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/68d7496d-c6b9-4fb9-878b-894b6fa5e929/adfm202307061-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c4e6f487-2e98-4a67-8c73-11689e2940d7/adfm202307061-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307061-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations and solvation structures of electrolytes with various components. (a) The binding energy of various components with Na<sup>+</sup>/PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in electrolytes. (b) Calculated HOMO energy levels of different components. (c) Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) (inset) curves of different electrolytes at room temperature. Coordination numbers of (d) LE, (e) LE+TFPBA and (f) GPE+TFPBA obtained from MD simulations and corresponding schematic diagrams showing the changes of the solvation structures from SSIP (LE) to CIP (GPE+TFPBA). (g) Raman spectra, (h) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR and (i) <sup>19</sup>F NMR of different electrolytes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were performed to analyze the solvation structure of different electrolyte systems. The calculated radial distribution functions and coordination numbers of electrolyte components are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2d–f</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13–S15</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). In the first solvation shell of Na<sup>+</sup> for LE system (around 2.95 Å, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information), the total coordination numbers of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2d</a>) with carbonate solvents (PC and EMC) is 3.57, which is much higher than that of Na<sup>+</sup>–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> (2.79). This trend suggests the solvent-separated ion pair (SSIP) structure associating with the heterogeneous plating of Na<sup>+</sup> and dissolution of cathode materials is formed in LE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0013_R_d131054337e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy Lett.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the polymer molecules and TFPBA with lower Na<sup>+</sup>-binding energy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2b</a>) can insert the first solvation shell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b–d</a>, Supporting Information), thus the coordination numbers with PC/EMC in LE+TFPBA, GPE, and GPE+TFPBA decrease gradually, which are 3.37, 3.02, and 2.96, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2e,f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), and lead to a higher fraction of free solvents in GPE+TFPBA. Moreover, the coordination number of Na<sup>+</sup>–PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in GPE+TFPBA is further increased to 3.23, corresponding to the contact ion pair (CIP) solvation structure<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0014_R_d131054337e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which often appears in high-salt-concentration electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Raman and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) characterizations were also carried out to provide further evidence for the changes in solvation structures. The Raman spectra shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2g</a> indicate that the fraction of free solvents in GPE+TFPBA is higher than that in LE and LE+TFPBA, suggesting that the P(ETPTA-AAEM) chains can weaken the interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and carbonate solvents and ensure a more efficient desolvation process, which can also be confirmed by the MD results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2e,f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) and the downshift of <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2h</a>). The upshift of <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra in LE+TFPBA and GPE+TFPBA indicates that the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions are anchored (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2i</a>), and it can be inferred that TFPBA dominates this process based on the result of theoretical calculations and experimental characterization, which can promote the dissociation of Na<sup>+</sup> and the reduce side reactions caused by PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0015_R_d131054337e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202307061-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties and Film-Forming Capability</h3>
            
            <p>To emphasize the positive effects of electrical coupling effect in electrolyte systems, comprehensive electrochemical tests of NLNM||Na batteries are conducted within the voltage range of 1.8–4.2&nbsp;V. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), the NLNM||LE||Na delivers the highest initial capacity of 144.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, but drops down rapidly after 50th cycle and remains a capacity retention of only 10.9% (15.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 550 cycles. In contrast, though NLNM||LE+TFPBA||Na cell displays relatively higher capacity retention of 61.9% (71.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 550 cycles, it shows the lowest initial discharge capacity (116.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and average CE (&lt;95.0% during the early 50 cycles) due to the severe decomposition of electrolyte. While benefiting from the better oxidation stability of GPE+TFPBA, the NLNM||GPE+TFPBA||Na cell exhibits excellent cycling performances with a capacity retention up to 71.0% (89.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and the highest average CE above 99.5% after 550 cycles. The lowest current in chronoamperometric profiles for GPE+TFPBA further demonstrates the highly oxidation stability at high voltages, which is in sharp contrast with the poor stability of LE+TFPBA due to the high reactivity of free TFPBA (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0016_R_d131054337e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the charge–discharge curves of the first five cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3c–e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information), a small platform can be observed at ≈4.0&nbsp;V in GPE+TFPBA from first to third cycle and disappears from fourth cycle, which is attributed to the well-tuned oxidation stability of TFPBA. In the cells using LE+TFPBA, however, the disappeared platform of TFPBA-oxidation may be attributed to the constant consumption of TFPBA from a low working voltage (≈3.2&nbsp;V, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0002">2c</a>) and the overlaps of phase-change platform at high voltages, which reflects in the shorter slopes between 3.0 and 3.3&nbsp;V during the first three cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3d</a>). And the voltage plateaus below 2&nbsp;V become shorter after the introduction of TFPBA and gel polymer network as seen in the initial charge–discharge curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3c–e</a>) owing to high stability at the expense of slightly initial capacity loss (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3a</a>), which would recover and maintain for extended cycles as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the cells using GPE+TFPBA show better structural reversibility, less charge–discharge polarization and voltage attenuation according to the selected charge–discharge curves, CV curves and EIS plots (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17–S20</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the NLNM||GPE+TFPBA||Na cell also delivers superior rate capability due to the facilitated ionic transfer and less polarization, with the discharge capacities of 131.5, 137.2, 121.8, 106.2, 95.4, 77.4, and 128.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> upon increasing rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2 C, 5, and back to 0.1 C, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307061-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a96328e6-6a5f-4af8-ba98-cad4e7ea75ae/adfm202307061-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a96328e6-6a5f-4af8-ba98-cad4e7ea75ae/adfm202307061-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f226693-5a2e-4dd1-931c-9cda03ddfae8/adfm202307061-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a96328e6-6a5f-4af8-ba98-cad4e7ea75ae/adfm202307061-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307061-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of half cells in various electrolytes. (a) Cyclic performances of NLNM||Na cells employing different electrolytes. (b) Chronoamperometric profiles of NLNM||Na cells in different electrolytes at 4.2&nbsp;V after three initial cycles at 0.2 C between 1.8 and 4.2&nbsp;V. Charge-discharge curves of (c) NLNM||LE||Na, (d) NLNM||LE+TFPBA||Na and (e) NLNM||GPE+TFPBA||Na cell from 1<sup>st</sup> to 5<sup>th</sup> cycle. The TEM images of NLNM particles after standing for 20&nbsp;h before cycling (f) and after 400 cycles using different electrolytes (g).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electrochemical performance of SIBs is closely related to the film-forming capability of electrolytes on the cathode surface. As displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20 and S22</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3f</a>, TFPBA can absorb spontaneously onto the cathodes and form a uniform CEI layer before cycling. And benefiting from the immobilized-TFPBA within P(ETPTA-AAEM) network via electrical coupling effect, the pre-oxidation of GPE+TFPBA has been well-tuned and the spontaneous CEI film is thinner and more uniform before cycling. In addition, the CEI in GPE+TFPBA remains smooth after first cycle, while the CEI in LE thickens unevenly (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). Not surprisingly, after 400 cycles a thick (&gt;20&nbsp;nm) and uneven CEI layer could be observed on the surface of cathode using LE, which mainly derived from the decomposition of carbonates (e.g., PC and EMC) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information, and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0003">3g</a>). And the cathodes using LE+TFPBA present an excessively thick CEI film (≈15&nbsp;nm) due to the excessive decomposition of electrolyte, leading to deterioration of electrochemical performance. Notably, the growth rates of CEI layers derived from gel electrolytes are greatly reduced, and in particular, a uniform CEI layer with an average thickness of only 7.5&nbsp;nm could be observed in GPE+TFPBA, which ensures good cyclic stability and excellent rate performance for the cells.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202307061-sec-0050-title">2.3 Failure Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>In order to obtain insight into the failure mechanism of NLNMs, postmortem analyses of NLNMs after 400 cycles were performed using scanning electron microscope (SEM), high-angle annular dark field-scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM), and inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES). In contrast to pristine NLNMs with clean and smooth surface before cycling, the cathodes cycled in LE, LE+TFPBA and GPE present various degrees of degradation, showing stepped morphology and coarse surfaces caused by lattice slip and associated by-products generation, which are not evident in GPE+TFPBA (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25 and S26</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, as shown in the HAADF-STEM images of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0004">4a–c</a>, numerous intragranular cracks could be observed in the cathode particles cycled in LE, and dense lattice mismatches and defects are induced from the surface to the interior of particles to cause the impeded Na<sup>+</sup> transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0017_R_d131054337e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While in the cathodes using LE+TFPBA (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27b</a>, Supporting Information, and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0004">4d–f</a>), the structural degradation has been mitigated, but some microcracks still initiate at the edge. In sharp contrast, the particles using GPE+TFPBA show an intact and smooth morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27c</a>, Supporting Information), and an ordered layered structure is maintained both near the interface and inside the NLNMs due to the strengthening protection of the CEI film (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0004">4g–i</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). The microscopic observations are further supported by the ICP-AES tests of dissolved TM ions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). Evidently, the NLNMs using GPE+TFPBA have suffered the lightest corrosion and TM dissolution. It is thus reasonable to conclude that GPE+TFPBA undergoes less decomposition during the cycling and the stable CEI film can resist the attack of acidic substances, which is beneficial for the long-term cycling stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307061-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b023983a-fa7a-4cb2-a24e-ce5763c64584/adfm202307061-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b023983a-fa7a-4cb2-a24e-ce5763c64584/adfm202307061-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c829652-e98e-461b-8f36-adb0eb7d382a/adfm202307061-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b023983a-fa7a-4cb2-a24e-ce5763c64584/adfm202307061-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307061-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Failure Analysis of NLNM cathodes in different electrolytes. The STEM images of NLNM after 400 cycles using LE, LE+TFPBA and GPE+TFPBA.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202307061-sec-0060-title">2.4 Interfacial Analysis and CEI Formation Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>In-depth X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were applied to analyze the chemical compositions and to unravel the formation mechanism of CEI. According to the integrated analysis at various etching depth, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5a,c</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30–S32</a> (Supporting Information), adverse inorganic segments<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0018_R_d131054337e931" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (e.g., Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>PO<i><sub>y</sub></i>F<i><sub>z</sub></i>, P─O, P─O─F, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, MnF<sub>2</sub>/NiF<sub>2</sub>, and P─F) and unstable organic segments decomposed from carbonate solvents (e.g., phosphates, carbonates, RONa, and ─COONa) dominant the CEI film originating from LE. While in LE+TFPBA and GPE+TFPBA, B-containing species associating with the oxidation of TFPBA can be clearly detected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5b,d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30 and S32</a>, Supporting Information). In general, some B-containing substances (e.g., fluoroboron complexes and borates) with poor crystallinity, potential uniform ion flux, and favorable flexibility,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0019_R_d131054337e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Energy">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can cover the particles entirely and accommodate the volume change for the lattice upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0018_R_d131054337e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0020_R_d131054337e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochem. Commun.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there are still some detrimental by-products generated by the oxidation of TFPBA, such as BF<sub>3</sub> and phenol analogs, which tend to dissolve in electrolyte to catalyze organic reactions and corrode cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307061-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307061-bib-0021_R_d131054337e974" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Polymer Chem. Heterocycl. Compd.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Apparently, in LE+TFPBA, there are no B─C peaks at ≈188.5&nbsp;eV in the P 2s/B 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32c</a>, Supporting Information), and as the depth increases, the concentration of the final oxidation products (P-containing borates at ≈135.0&nbsp;eV and B─O segments at ≈191.6&nbsp;eV) remains high, while the peaks related to transition states (B─F segments ≈687.1&nbsp;eV) are still weak. This tendency infers that TFPBA with high oxidative instability has been completely oxidized in LE+TFPBA during cycling (Step 1 and Step 2 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5e</a>), which leads to the formation of inactive and low-modulus CEI film with unstable components (BF<sub>3</sub> and phenol analogs) and the poor electrochemical performance with fluctuation.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307061-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c0f6cecb-31fb-499e-9c91-b1595f767ed1/adfm202307061-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c0f6cecb-31fb-499e-9c91-b1595f767ed1/adfm202307061-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4caba7aa-3eec-424d-81c8-8cd7925b217b/adfm202307061-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c0f6cecb-31fb-499e-9c91-b1595f767ed1/adfm202307061-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307061-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Interfacial analyses and CEI formation mechanism. XPS depth profiles of (a) F 1s in LE, (b) F 1s in GPE+TFPBA, (c) P 2s in LE and (d) P 2s/B 1s in GPE+TFPBA. (e) Construction processes of CEI from TFPBA as additive. Schematic illustration of CEIs in (f) LE and (g) GPE+TFPBA (the species with light colors are regarded as robust components in CEI layers).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As for GPE+TFPBA, the introduction of polymer network with enriched polar groups can regulate the oxidative stability of TFPBA via electrical coupling effect, and more favorable CEI components can be obtained. First, more species containing B─C bond (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5d</a>) could be observed due to the restricted oxidation process (Step 1, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5e</a>), and the generation of detrimental BF<sub>3</sub> and phenol analogs are effectively suppressed. Secondly, this well-modulated electrolyte participates in the formation of CEI film containing robust fluoroboron complexes and moderate borates during the cycles, which ensures the long-term stability of the batteries. Collectively, compared with other electrolyte systems (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information), due to the well-regulated oxidation process of TFPBA and the optimized CIP solvation structure for GPE+TFPBA, the surface of the cathodes using GPE+TFPBA has formed an organic–inorganic hybrid CEI film containing favorable fluoroboron complexes, NaF and other inorganic species (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307061-fig-0005">5g</a>). Besides, the mechanical properties of CEI layers after 400 cycles were also tested by atomic force microscopy (AFM). As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> (Supporting Information), the GPE+TFPBA-derived CEI has a higher modulus (2.54&nbsp;GPa) and a lower surface roughness (680&nbsp;nm) than those of LE (1.92&nbsp;GPa, 851&nbsp;nm). It further confirms a more uniform and robust protective layer on NLNM cycling in GPE+TFPBA, which plays a key role in enhancing the electrochemical performance of high-voltage SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307061-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307061-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we demonstrated an electrical coupling mechanism to optimize electrolyte system consisted of highly-active TFPBA additive and P(ETPTA-AAEM)-based polymer network. Through the strong electrical coupling effect, polymer chains with electronegative groups can regulate the solvation structure of electrolytes from SSIP to CIP, and immobilize TFPBA to enhance its oxidative stability. Moreover, TFPBA with electron acceptor function also can spontaneously adsorb onto the surface of the cathodes to participate in the formation of the CEI film and play a key role in fixing PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> to inhibit their excessive decomposition. Under the synergy effect of the electrical-coupled GPE+TFPBA electrolyte, a thin and robust organic-inorganic hybrid CEI film containing favorable fluoroboron complexes and NaF is formed on the surface of cathodes. As a result, the NLNM||GPE+TFPBA||Na cell reveals an impressive long-term capacity retention of 71.0% over 550 cycles and a good rate capability up to 5 C. We believe that this work provides a new insight for rational and efficient use of highly active additives to enhance the electrochemical performance of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
